        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c17554"><meta property="og:title" content="O3-Type Na2/3Ni1/3Ti2/3O2 Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/acsami.1c17554.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are currently the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage devices owing to their low cost and abundant resources. Titanium-based layered oxides have attracted widespread attention as promising anode materials due to delivering a safe potential of about 0.7 V (vs Na+/Na) and a small volume contraction during cycles; P2-type Ti-based layered oxides are typically reported, due to the challenging synthesis of the O3-type counterpart resulting from the high percentage of unstable Ti3+. Herein, we report an anomalous O3–Na2/3Ni1/3Ti2/3O2 layered oxide as an ultrastable and high-rate anode material for SIBs. The anode material delivers a reversible capacity of 112 mA h g–1 after 300 cycles at 0.1 C, a good capacity retention rate of 91% after 1400 cycles at 2 C, and, in particular, a capacity of 52 mA h g–1 even at a high rate of 20 C (1780 mA g–1). Furthermore, the in situ X-ray diffraction monitoring reveals no phase transitions and almost zero strain both underlie the good long-cycle stability. The measured high apparent Na+ diffusion coefficient (2.06 × 10–10 cm2 s–1) and the low migration energy barrier (0.59 eV) from density functional theory calculations are responsible for the superior rate capability. Our results promise advanced high-performance O3-type Ti-based layered oxides as promising anode materials toward application for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/acsami.1c17554.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da889b2c7fb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da889b2c7fb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '43e25b1d-e80d-4fbc-b0e8-3c9f0e83d456'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};

                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da889b2c7fb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90da889b2c7fb325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "O3-Type Na&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ni&lt;sub&gt;1/3&lt;/sub&gt;Ti&lt;sub&gt;2/3&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Layered Oxide as a Stable and High-Rate Anode Material for Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c17554", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Xin</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Sen Xin</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">CAS Key Laboratory of Molecular Nanostructure and Nanotechnology, Beijing National Laboratory for Molecular Science, Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS), Beijing 100190, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Sen++Xin" data-id="article_author_search">Sen Xin</a></span></div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D677%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DSi-Yuan%2BZhang%252C%2BYa-Nan%2BZhou%252C%2BLianzheng%2BYu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c17554%26title%3DO3-Type%2BNa2%252F3Ni1%252F3Ti2%252F3O2%2BLayered%2BOxide%2Bas%2Ba%2BStable%2Band%2BHigh-Rate%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D683%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2022" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are currently the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage devices owing to their low cost and abundant resources. Titanium-based layered oxides have attracted widespread attention as promising anode materials due to delivering a safe potential of about 0.7 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and a small volume contraction during cycles; P2-type Ti-based layered oxides are typically reported, due to the challenging synthesis of the O3-type counterpart resulting from the high percentage of unstable Ti<sup>3+</sup>. Herein, we report an anomalous O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered oxide as an ultrastable and high-rate anode material for SIBs. The anode material delivers a reversible capacity of 112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.1 C, a good capacity retention rate of 91% after 1400 cycles at 2 C, and, in particular, a capacity of 52 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at a high rate of 20 C (1780 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Furthermore, the <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction monitoring reveals no phase transitions and almost zero strain both underlie the good long-cycle stability. The measured high apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (2.06 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) and the low migration energy barrier (0.59 eV) from density functional theory calculations are responsible for the superior rate capability. Our results promise advanced high-performance O3-type Ti-based layered oxides as promising anode materials toward application for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D677%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DSi-Yuan%2BZhang%252C%2BYa-Nan%2BZhou%252C%2BLianzheng%2BYu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c17554%26title%3DO3-Type%2BNa2%252F3Ni1%252F3Ti2%252F3O2%2BLayered%2BOxide%2Bas%2Ba%2BStable%2Band%2BHigh-Rate%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D683%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291484&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffraction" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffraction" class="article__tags__link">Diffraction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ti-based+Layered+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Ti-based%20layered%20oxides">Ti-based layered oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long+Cycling+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long%20cycling%20life">long cycling life</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20rate%20capability">high rate capability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Exploring promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) is of urgency in the field of large-scale storage of renewable energy, due to the ever-increasing price of lithium sources from the rapidly growing demand on portable electronic devices and electric vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Among these energy storage devices that use clean energies such as wind, water, and solar energy, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been the most promising candidate, owing to the abundant Na<sup>+</sup> resources and low price, as well as a mechanism similar to that of LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 5 to 9">(5−9)</a> For different types of anode materials, there are still many problems which have seriously hindered the practical commercialization of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> Alloy anode materials (such as P, Sb, and Sn) have difficulty in lowering their huge volume changes during the cycles, albeit possessing high capacities (more than 600 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> Organic anode materials also suffer from poor cycling and rate performance due to their low conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> Carbon materials are currently the main anode materials due to their advantages of high capacity and good cyclability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> However, the majority of the capacity of hard carbon comes from a low voltage plateau below 0.1 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The low voltage could raise safety concerns regarding the formation of sodium dendrite at fast charging rates or overcharging process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> In addition, the rate performance of the hard carbon anode is not satisfactory. Therefore, anode materials with a long lifespan and good safety are vital to fulfilling the application of SIBs toward grid energy storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">Titanium-based layered oxide has received widespread attention because of the appropriate working potential (e.g., 0.7 V) based on the Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> redox potential (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and nontoxicity. Previously reported Ti-based layered anode materials involved Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Li<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.78</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mg<sub>1/6</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> and Na<sub>0.62</sub>Ti<sub>0.37</sub>Cr<sub>0.63</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> most of which belong to P2-type structures. In marked contrast to the P2-type structure, O3–NaTiO<sub>2</sub> was reported recently to deliver a high reversible capacity of 152 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which was higher than those of the P2-type counterparts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> However, the metastable Ti<sup>3+</sup> in O3–NaTiO<sub>2</sub> incurs both a challenging synthesis procedure and an undesired O3–O′3 phase transition. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> One effective improvement strategy, by introducing metal cations below +3 into Ti sites, is used to increase the valence state of Ti<sup>3+</sup> to Ti<sup>4+</sup> and stabilize O3-type compounds. For instance, Li<sup>+</sup> was introduced into Ti sites to obtain O3-[Na<sub>0.73</sub>Li<sub>0.09</sub>][Li<sub>0.27</sub>Ti<sub>0.36</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> was used to obtain a low Na content O3–Na<sub>0.66</sub>Mg<sub>0.34</sub>Ti<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> anode material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> These O3-type anode materials all experienced a single-phase reaction without the presence of a new phase during cycles and also possessed good air stability. These features are different from the complex phase transitions and poor air stability of the O3-type oxides reported previously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a> Therefore, an O3-type Ti-based layered oxide, if rationally introduced in other low-valence cations, is expected to deliver high electrochemical performances and further advance the development of SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we synthesized an anomalous O3-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered oxide as an anode material for SIBs. The O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits a reversible discharge capacity of 112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an average voltage of 0.7 V, and a superior rate capability with a long lifespan. The <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD) result, the high apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, and the low migration energy barrier, experimentally and theoretically augment the ultrastability and high rate capability of the O3-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> anode material for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The layered O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> anode material was prepared by a conventional sol–gel method as reported previously by our group. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Specifically, NaNO<sub>3</sub> (Alfa Aesar, 99.0%), Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>•6H<sub>2</sub>O (Alfa Aesar, 98.0%), and C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>36</sub>O<sub>4</sub>Ti (Aladdin, 99.0%) were dissolved in deionized water in a stoichiometric ratio, followed by dropwise addition of citric acid aqueous solution under stirring. The above solution was evaporated in a 70 °C water bath to form a gel and dried in vacuum at 120 °C for 72 h. After the precursor was initially calcined in air at 450 °C for 6 h and then at 700 °C for 12 h in air, O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was obtained by grinding the calcined product into a powder and storing in a glovebox with argon.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The XRD pattern was obtained on a Bruker D8 ADVANCE diffractometer operating with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5418 Å) in the 2θ range from 10 to 120° at a scan speed of 1° min<sup>–1</sup>. Based on the Rietveld method, TOPAS software was used to refine the XRD patterns. Using a scanning electron microscope (S4800) with a working voltage of 10 kV, the morphological characteristics of the materials were observed. The microstructure and element information of the layered structure were studied by high-resolution transition electron microscopy (HRTEM) and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). The high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and annular bright field (ABF) imaging were executed using a JEOL ARM200F (JEOL, Tokyo, Japan) scanning transition electron microscope. The changes in charge and discharge structures at 0.1 C were monitored using <i>in situ</i> XRD in the 2θ range of 10–80°. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250XI) was used to determine the element and its valence state of the transition metal.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Testing</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For electrochemical testing, two types of cells, CR2032 coin and Swagelok (for <i>in situ</i> XRD) cells, were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, &lt; 0.1 ppm). The anodes were prepared by uniformly mixing the active material, Super P carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride into a slurry at a mass ratio of 65:25:10, followed by coating on the aluminum foil and drying in vacuum for 12 h. The mass loading of the active material on the electrode is 2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The assembly was carried out using 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene and diethyl carbonate (1: 1 in volume, 5% FEC) as an electrolyte, porous glass fiber (GF/D) as the separator, and sodium metal as the counter electrode. The assembled 2032-type cells were examined for an electrochemical test in the LAND CT2001A battery test system. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were automatically collected using an electrochemical workstation (Princeton).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (abbreviated O3-NNT)-layered compound was synthesized using a sol–gel method reported in our recent study. O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is more thermodynamically stable compared with P3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, so the anomalous O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is generated at 700 °C, albeit its sodium content is less than 0.8. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a shows the XRD reflection peaks of O3-NNT with a space group of <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Furthermore, the refined crystallographic data show that the lattice structures are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.974(2) Å, <i>c</i> = 16.5198(5) Å, and <i>V</i> = 126.5879(6) Å<sup>3</sup> with a reliability index <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 4.6% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The crystal structure diagram shows that the structure of O3-NNT is stacked alternately by NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, with an ABCABC stacking pattern of the oxygen layer (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). In the O3-type structure, the NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra is arranged by sharing six edges with TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra of two adjacent layers. The size of the TMO<sub>2</sub> sheet (slabs) and the d-spacing of the sodium-ion diffusion layer (interslabs) are 2.05 and 3.45 Å, respectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Powder XRD and Rietveld refinement patterns of the O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> sample. (b) Schematic diagram of the crystal structure obtained by refining the data.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the O3-NNT was imaged by using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and TEM. The O3-NNT exhibits a morphology of irregular particles with the dimensional sizes ranging between 200 and 500 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). The HRTEM image shows a lattice fringe spacing of 5.5 Å, corresponding to the (003) plane (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). Furthermore, the atomic structural information of the O3-NNT material was probed using atomic-resolution scanning transition electron microscopy (STEM with ABF and HAADF). The interval of the continuous interference fringe is 5.5 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c), well consistent with that observed in the above HRTEM image. The bright-dot contrasts in the HAADF-STEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) and the dark dot contrasts in the ABF-STEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) reveal the column positions of Ti and Ni atoms. The dark-dot contrast with the interlayer positions in the ABF-STEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) corresponds to the atom column positions of Na and oxygen along the [010] zone axis. Moreover, the TEM/EDS images show that the uniformly distributed elements of Na, Ni, Ti, and O are in the material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). In addition, the element analysis by inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) reveals that the Na/Ni/Ti atomic ratio is 0.658:0.335:0.663, closely approximate to the theoretical value of O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM image and (b) HRTEM image of O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2.</sub> (c) HAADF image and (d) ABF images of a sample oriented in the [010] axis, corresponding to the atom model. (e) TEM/EDS maps of Na, Ni, Ti, and O elements of O3–Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the O3-NNT was examined in the half cell with Na foil as a counter electrode. During the voltage range of 0.05–2.0 V at a current rate of 0.1 C, the O3-NNT shows a reversible capacity of 112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a sodium storage voltage of 0.75 V corresponding to the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). The average voltage plateau is higher than the sodium plating voltage (below 0 V), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> which can promise good safety. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref39" aria-label="reference 28 and 39">(28,39)</a> No obvious capacity decay is observed after 300 cycles, suggesting good cyclic stability. Note that the sodium contents of these conventional O3-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> materials are typically <i>x</i> ≥ 0.8; however, in our case, the O3-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> anode material has a low Na content (0.67) and possesses an available vacancy of 0.33 for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performances of O3-NNT in the half cell. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge of the half-cell during a voltage window of 0.05–2 V after 300 cycles at 0.1 C. (b) Rate performance of the O3-NNT electrode with increasing current densities from 0.1 to 20 C. (c) Cycling performance after 1400 cycles at 2 C in a voltage range of 0.05–2 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Generally, the low sodium content endows the O3-NNT with two advantageous features: (i) enough sodium vacancy to embed sodium ions and (ii) a wide spacing of 3.45 Å, which is conducive to the diffusion of sodium ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> In our case, a reversible capacity of 112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to 0.41 Na insertion/extraction per formula unit. Furthermore, the O3-NNT shows an excellent capacity retention of 85.6% after 300 cycles at 0.1 C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The cycling stability is in good agreement with the well-matched CV curves throughout the first three cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The result demonstrates that the O3-NNT cycled between 0.05 and 2 V has one pair of the reversible Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple, which is indeed consistent with the redox peaks of 0.75 and 0.65 V in the CV curves. Note that a low initial Coulombic efficiency of 46% was observed in the first cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The result is closely related with the large irreversible sodium insertion into a carbon additive and generally is ascribed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase layer on the surface of NNT as reported perviously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 41 to 43">(41−43)</a> Considering the low electronic conductivity of the Ti-based oxide anode materials and the large irreversible capacity from Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into carbon black, it is thus important for the Ti-based oxide anode material to promote the electrode conductivity through designing the Ti-based oxide composite material with good conductivity or formulating the carbon conductive additive with graphene or carbon nanotubes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b shows that the O3-NNT material exhibits highly reversible capacities of 115, 105, 96, 90, 84, 80, 76, 66, 56, and 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> as increasing current densities from 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5, 10, to 15 C, and even to 20 C, respectively. In particular, the capacity retention at 5 C reaches 66% initial capacity at 0.1 C. Furthermore, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c shows that the O3-NNT material exhibits an ultralong cycling stability of 1400 cycles at 2 C. These results demonstrate the superior rate capability and long-life cyclic stability of the O3-NNT.</div><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the good rate capability, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of the O3-NNT electrode was evaluated using CV experiments at different scanning speeds between 0.05 and 2 V. The Randles–Sevcik equation is shown by the expression <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where, <i>I</i><sub>P</sub> is the current peak (A), <i>n</i> is the number of transferring electrons in the reaction, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant (96.485 C k mol<sup>–1</sup>), <i>C</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration (mol cm<sup>–3</sup>) in the electrode, <i>S</i> is the area (cm<sup>2</sup>) of the electrode, <i>R</i> is the gas constant (8.314 J mol<sup>–1</sup> K<sup>–1</sup>), <i>D</i><sub>cv</sub> is the Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficient (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), and <i>v</i> is the scanning rate (V s<sup>–1</sup>). The Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>cv</sub>) of O3-NNT was calculated to be 2.06 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), which is almost 2 orders of magnitude higher than most sodium-based layered oxides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><sup>]</sup> The value reflects a rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and strongly underpins the good rate performance. At the same time, according to the linear fitting of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, the value of parameters b is 0.88, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c</a>. This electrode thus has both diffusion-controlled and capacitive responses.</div><div class="NLM_p">Generally, the rate capability of the layered oxide cathode materials greatly depends on the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in SIBs. To get insights into the rate capability of the O3-NNT, the first-principles calculation was used with the nudged elastic band (NEB) method. The calculated activation barrier energy for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the <i>ab</i> plane of the structure is only ≈0.59 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), which is lower than that of the previously reported NaTiO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> and indeed enables fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into the structure, and thereby proves the high rate capability of O3-NNT.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) NEB calculation of activation barrier energy for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in O3-NNT. (b) Schematic diagram of diffusion pathways of sodium ions in the O3-NNT structure.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further test the structure and the air stability, aging testing was performed for O3-NNT materials including the pristine, the post-cycled after 700 cycles, and the exposed-to-air for 7 days. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows that either after 700 cycles or after 7 days of exposure to air, the XRD patterns of the post-cycled and the exposed-to-air O3-NNT materials are the same as the pristine O3-NNT in terms of peak location (2θ). In addition, the exposed-to-air O3-NNT shows quite the same as those of the pristine O3-NNT. All the results strongly suggest good structural and air stability of the O3-NNT.</div><div class="NLM_p">The compensation mechanism and capacity contribution of the O3-NNT were probed using <i>ex situ</i> XPS. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows that in the initial state the peaks observed at 459.2 and 465.3 eV are visible, which correspond to Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, and reflect the presence of Ti<sup>4+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> When the discharge reaches 0.05 V, the Ti 2p peak shifts to low energy, and a new Ti<sup>3+</sup> peak appears at 457.5 eV. When the charge increases to 2 V, all peaks return to their original positions. By contrast, no shift of the Ni<sup>2+</sup> peaks was found during the whole charging and discharging processes, which strongly suggests no redox reaction of Ni. The result demonstrates that the O3-NNT cycled between 0.05 and 2 V only has Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couples, which is indeed consistent with a peak of 0.75 V in the CV curve.</div><div class="NLM_p">The phase evolution of the O3-NNT was monitored using <i>in situ</i> XRD during the insertion and extraction of sodium ions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). The continued intercalation of sodium ions leads to the decrease in the layer spacing corresponding to the slight shift of the (003) peak to a high-angle shift during a discharging process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> After charging to 2 V, all the diffraction peaks return to their original positions, and no new peaks appear during the whole process. By refining the results of <i>in situ</i> XRD, the change of the unit cell parameters is intuitively shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c–e. The maximum change in the unit cell volume is only 1.1%, indicating the zero-strain feature of the O3-NNT. The <i>in situ</i> XRD result further reveals the characteristics of no phase transition and almost zero strain of O3-NNT materials during the charge and discharge, which is responsible for the long-cycle stability.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/medium/am1c17554_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) <i>In situ</i> XRD patterns collected during the second discharge/charge process of O3-NNT under a current rate of 0.1 C at voltage range between 0.05 and 2 V. (b) Corresponding intensity contour maps concerning the evolution of the diffraction peaks of 10–80°. (c–e) Evolution of lattice parameters for the O3-NNT electrode from <i>in situ</i> XRD measurements during the charge/discharge process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/asset/images/large/am1c17554_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17554&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we have successfully demonstrated the air-stable O3-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layer oxide as the anode material for SIBs. The anode material delivered a high reversible capacity (112mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 0.1 C) and a safe average working voltage of 0.75 V, as well as, in particular, an excellent rate capability (52 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 C). Furthermore, no phase transitions and the almost zero strain revealed by <i>in situ</i> XRD monitoring underlie the good long-cycle stability; while both the quantitatively determined high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 2.06 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and the theoretically calculated migration energy barrier of 0.59 eV underpinned the superior rate capability. The results, together with the unique and low-sodium content O3-type structure, can promise the advancement of high-performance O3-layered oxide anode materials toward their further application for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17554?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17554</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD Rietveld refinement results, ICP-AES result, cycling performance, CV curve, XRD patterns, and <i>ex situ</i> XPS spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17554/suppl_file/am1c17554_si_001.pdf">am1c17554_si_001.pdf (558.07 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c17554" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVKnsb3K&amp;md5=69dbcead9b26c0f562141f9a6dfd074d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4');
